	<div class="vector-dropdown-content">


					
<div id="p-variants" class="vector-menu mw-portlet mw-portlet-variants emptyPortlet"  >
	<div class="vector-menu-content">
		
		<ul class="vector-menu-content-list">
			
			
		</ul>
		
	</div>

				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>
					<div class="vector-body-before-content">
							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Category of memory stabilizing processes</div>
<p><b>Memory consolidation</b> is a category of biological processes in the brain that stabilize a memory over time after it is initially learned.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dudai&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dudai-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  A memory trace is the biological change in neural circuits caused by acquisition of new information. Consolidation is distinguished into two specific processes. The first, <b>synaptic consolidation</b>, which is thought to correspond to <a href="/wiki/Long-term_potentiation#Late_phase" title="Long-term potentiation">late-phase long-term potentiation</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bramham&#95;2005&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bramham_2005-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> occurs on a small scale in the synaptic connections and neural circuits within the first few hours after learning. The second process is <b>systems consolidation</b>, occurring on a much larger scale in the brain, with gradual reorganization and reduced reliance on the hippocampus and strengthening of distributed cortical networks over a period of weeks to years. This involves several mechanisms including molecular, synaptic, and systems-level processes that stabilize the <a href="/wiki/Neuronal_ensemble" title="Neuronal ensemble">neural ensemble</a> (<a href="/wiki/Engram_(neuropsychology)" title="Engram (neuropsychology)">engram</a>) that is thought to represent the newly formed memory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Recently, a third process has become the focus of research, <b>reconsolidation</b>, in which previously consolidated memories can be made <a href="/wiki/Lability" title="Lability">labile</a> again through reactivation of the memory trace.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nader&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nader-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sara&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sara-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Memory_consolidation.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="position of consolidation to the information-to-memory process" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1f/Memory_consolidation.svg/960px-Memory_consolidation.svg.png" decoding="async" width="575" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1f/Memory_consolidation.svg/1280px-Memory_consolidation.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="737" data-file-height="212" /></a><figcaption>The line processes to make information memory</figcaption></figure>
<p>Memory consolidation was first referred to in the writings of the renowned <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> teacher of <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a> <a href="/wiki/Quintillian" class="mw-redirect" title="Quintillian">Quintillian</a>.  He noted the "curious fact... that the interval of a single night will greatly increase the strength of the memory," and presented the possibility that "... the power of recollection .. undergoes a process of ripening and maturing during the time which intervenes."  The process of consolidation was later proposed based on clinical data illustrated in 1882 by <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9odule-Armand_Ribot" title="Théodule-Armand Ribot">Ribot's</a> <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Regression" class="mw-redirect" title="Law of Regression">Law of Regression</a>, "progressive destruction advances progressively from the unstable to the stable". This idea was elaborated on by William H. Burnham a few years later in a paper on amnesia integrating findings from experimental psychology and neurology.  Coining of the term "consolidation" is credited to the German researchers <a href="/wiki/Georg_Elias_M%C3%BCller" title="Georg Elias Müller">Müller</a> and Alfons Pilzecker who rediscovered the concept that memory takes time to fixate or undergo "Konsolidierung" in their studies conducted between 1892 and 1900.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dudai&#95;1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dudai-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The two proposed the perseveration-consolidation <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothesis</a> after they found that new information learned could disrupt information previously learnt if not enough time had passed to allow the old information to be consolidated.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-JamesLMcGaugh&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JamesLMcGaugh-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This led to the suggestion that new memories are fragile in nature but as time passes they become solidified.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-JamesLMcGaugh&#95;6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JamesLMcGaugh-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Gray739-emphasizing-hippocampus.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Gray739-emphasizing-hippocampus.png/330px-Gray739-emphasizing-hippocampus.png" decoding="async" width="309" height="234" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/Gray739-emphasizing-hippocampus.png 1.5x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="379" /></a><figcaption>Lateral view of the hippocampus which is located in the medial temporal lobe</figcaption></figure><p>Systematic studies of <a href="/wiki/Anterograde_amnesia" title="Anterograde amnesia">anterograde amnesia</a> started to emerge in the 1960s and 1970s. The case of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Molaison" title="Henry Molaison">Henry Molaison</a>, formerly known as patient H.M., became a <a href="/wiki/Landmark" title="Landmark">landmark</a> in studies of memory as it relates to <a href="/wiki/Amnesia" title="Amnesia">amnesia</a> and the removal of the <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">hippocampal zone</a> and sparked massive interest in the study of <a href="/wiki/Lesions" class="mw-redirect" title="Lesions">brain lesions</a> and their effect on memory. After Molaison underwent a <a href="/wiki/Bilateral_symmetry" class="mw-redirect" title="Bilateral symmetry">bilateral</a> <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location#Left_and_right_(lateral),_and_medial" title="Anatomical terms of location">medial</a> <a href="/wiki/Temporal_lobe" title="Temporal lobe">temporal lobe</a> <a href="/wiki/Segmental_resection" title="Segmental resection">resection</a> to alleviate <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epileptic symptoms</a> the patient began to suffer from memory impairments. Molaison lost the ability to encode and consolidate newly learned information leading researchers to conclude the <a href="/wiki/Medial_temporal_lobe" class="mw-redirect" title="Medial temporal lobe">medial temporal lobe</a> (MTL) was an important <a href="/wiki/Structure" title="Structure">structure</a> involved in this process.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ScovilleMilner&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ScovilleMilner-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Molaison also showed signs of <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_amnesia" title="Retrograde amnesia">retrograde amnesia</a> spanning a period of about three years prior to the <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">surgery</a> suggesting that recently acquired memories of as long as a couple years could remain in the MTL prior to consolidation into other brain areas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Milner&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Milner-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Research into other patients with resections of the MTL have shown a positive relationship between the degree of memory impairment and the extent of MTL removal which points to a temporal <a href="/wiki/Gradient" title="Gradient">gradient</a> in the consolidating nature of the MTL.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ScovilleMilner&#95;7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ScovilleMilner-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>These studies were accompanied by the creation of animal models of human <a href="/wiki/Amnesia" title="Amnesia">amnesia</a> in an effort to identify brain substrates critical for slow consolidation.  Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Neuropsychopharmacology" title="Neuropsychopharmacology">neuropharmacological</a> studies of selected brain areas began to shed light on the <a href="/wiki/Molecules" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecules">molecules</a> possibly responsible for fast consolidation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dudai&#95;1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dudai-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In recent decades, advancements in <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cellular</a> preparations, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neurogenetics" title="Neurogenetics">neurogenetics</a> have revolutionized the study of consolidation. Providing additional support is the study of functional brain activity in humans which has revealed that the activity of brain regions changes over time after a new memory is acquired.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ScovilleMilner&#95;7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ScovilleMilner-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This change can occur as quickly as a couple hours after the memory has been <a href="/wiki/Encoding_(memory)" title="Encoding (memory)">encoded</a> suggesting that there is a <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">temporal</a> dimension to the reorganization of the memory as it is represented in the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-JamesLMcGaugh&#95;6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JamesLMcGaugh-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Synaptic_consolidation">Synaptic consolidation</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Synaptic consolidation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">Synaptic consolidation</a> is one form of memory consolidation seen across all species and long-term memory tasks. <a href="/wiki/Long-term_memory" title="Long-term memory">Long-term memory</a>, when discussed in the context of synaptic consolidation, is conventionally said to be <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a> that lasts for at least 24 hours.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dudai&#95;1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dudai-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Synaptic consolidation is achieved faster than systems consolidation (which is assumed to take weeks, months, or even to years to be accomplished<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dudai&#95;et&#95;al.&#95;2015&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dudai_et_al._2015-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>). There is evidence to suggest that synaptic consolidation takes place within minutes to hours of <a href="/wiki/Memory_encoding" class="mw-redirect" title="Memory encoding">memory encoding</a> or <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a> (shown, for example, in goldfish),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dudai&#95;1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dudai-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and as such it is considered the 'fast' type of consolidation. It is also referred to as 'initial consolidation'.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Okuda&#95;et&#95;al.&#95;2020&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Okuda_et_al._2020-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As soon as six hours after training, memories become impervious to interferences that disrupt synaptic consolidation and the formation of long-term memory. <a href="/wiki/Long-term_potentiation#Late_phase" title="Long-term potentiation">Late-phase LTP</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bramham&#95;2005&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bramham_2005-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the long-lasting form of one of the best understood forms of <a href="/wiki/Synaptic_plasticity" title="Synaptic plasticity">synaptic plasticity</a>, is thought to be the cellular process underlying synaptic consolidation.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Standard_model">Standard model</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Standard model"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The standard model of synaptic consolidation suggests that alterations of synaptic <a href="/wiki/Protein_synthesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Protein synthesis">protein synthesis</a> and resulting changes in <a href="/wiki/Membrane_potential" title="Membrane potential">membrane potential</a> are achieved through activating <a href="/wiki/Signal_transduction" title="Signal transduction">intracellular transduction cascades</a>. These molecular cascades trigger <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factors" class="mw-redirect" title="Transcription factors">transcription factors</a> that lead to changes in <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a>. The result of the gene expression is the lasting alteration of synaptic proteins, as well as synaptic remodeling and growth. In a short time-frame immediately following learning, the molecular cascade, expression and process of both transcription factors and <a href="/wiki/Immediate_early_genes" class="mw-redirect" title="Immediate early genes">immediate early genes</a>, are susceptible to disruptions.  Disruptions caused by specific drugs, antibodies and gross physical trauma can block the effects of synaptic consolidation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dudai&#95;1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dudai-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Synaptic_Plasticity">Synaptic Plasticity</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Synaptic Plasticity"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Additionally, synaptic consolidation is supported by multiple forms of <a href="/wiki/Synaptic_plasticity" title="Synaptic plasticity">synaptic plasticity</a>. Synaptic plasticity is largely defined as the ability of a neural connection to change in strength over time due to exposure to different levels of stimuli.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In other words, an organism's neural connections can adapt and change to accommodate different environmental stimuli which can have larger systemic effects. Some mechanistic examples of this include <a href="/wiki/Long-term_potentiation" title="Long-term potentiation">long-term potentiation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Long-term_depression" title="Long-term depression">long-term depression</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Long-term potentiation is an increase in the strength of a synaptic input as a result of repeated high-frequency stimulation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In other words, neural responses across two neurons strengthen over time the more those neurons fire. In contrast to long-term potentiation is long-term depression, which results from low-frequency or lack of stimulation. In conjunction, this means that synaptic connections between neurons can either increase or decrease in strength due to frequency or intensity of use. Global implications of this can be found in brain areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">hippocampus</a>, where one study on rabbits demonstrated that repeated stimulation of hippocampal neurons led to an increase in the future efficacy of their activation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Long-term_potentiation">Long-term potentiation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Long-term potentiation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Long-term_potentiation" title="Long-term potentiation">Long-term potentiation</a></div>
<p>LTP can be thought of as the prolonged strengthening of <a href="/wiki/Synaptic_transmission" class="mw-redirect" title="Synaptic transmission">synaptic transmission</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tronson&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tronson-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and is known to produce increases in the <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> production and <a href="/wiki/Receptor_(biochemistry)" title="Receptor (biochemistry)">receptor</a> sensitivity, lasting minutes to even days. The process of <a href="/wiki/Long-term_potentiation" title="Long-term potentiation">LTP</a> is regarded as a contributing factor to <a href="/wiki/Synaptic_plasticity" title="Synaptic plasticity">synaptic plasticity</a> and in the growth of <a href="/wiki/Synaptic_strength" class="mw-redirect" title="Synaptic strength">synaptic strength</a>, which are suggested to underlie memory formation. LTP is also considered to be an important mechanism in terms of maintaining memories within brain regions,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Spencer&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spencer-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and therefore is thought to be involved in learning.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tronson&#95;15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tronson-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is compelling evidence that LTP is critical for Pavlovian fear conditioning in rats suggesting that it mediates <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a> in mammals. Specifically, <a href="/wiki/NMDA-receptor" class="mw-redirect" title="NMDA-receptor">NMDA-receptor</a> <a href="/wiki/Receptor_antagonist" title="Receptor antagonist">antagonists</a> appear to block the induction of both LTP and <a href="/wiki/Fear_conditioning" title="Fear conditioning">fear conditioning</a> and that fear conditioning increases <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">amygdaloidal</a> synaptic transmission that would result in LTP.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Maren&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maren-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Structural_Plasticity">Structural Plasticity</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Structural Plasticity"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In order to discuss how memory consolidation works on a broader scale we have to also acknowledge the broader mechanisms of plasticity that occur outside of the synapse. This brings us to the process of <a href="/wiki/Structural_plasticity" class="mw-redirect" title="Structural plasticity">structural plasticity</a>. Structural plasticity represents changes in the broader cell, encompassing both changes in neuron shape, changes in supporting cell shape, and formation of new cells entirely.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Because memory consolidation involves the long-term stabilization of <a href="/wiki/Neural_circuit" title="Neural circuit">neural circuits</a>, these large-scale structural changes are thought to contribute to the persistence and organization of consolidated memories.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The following is a brief list of some of the most significant mechanisms of plasticity in the brain.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Neurogenesis">Neurogenesis</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Neurogenesis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neurogenesis" title="Neurogenesis">Neurogenesis</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Neurogenesis" title="Neurogenesis">Neurogenesis</a> which is the brains process of making new neurons. While this has not been demonstrable in adult humans<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>, neurogenesis is a major contributor to brain plasticity in other organisms such as rodents.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, it has been proposed that newly generated neurons in the hippocampus can integrate into memory-related circuits in newborns, and this process has been proposed to support certain forms of memory consolidation—particularly those involving pattern separation or long-term retention.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Synaptic_Remodelling">Synaptic Remodelling</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Synaptic Remodelling"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>However, this is not the only possible mechanism that enables structural changes in neurons. <a href="/wiki/Dendritic_spine" title="Dendritic spine">Dendritic spine</a> remodelling is one of the primary mechanisms by which neuroplasticity is derived. Dendritic spines are signal receiving locations on neurons found on <a href="/wiki/Dendrite" title="Dendrite">dendrites</a>. There are many types of spines that possess different functions, however the important aspect of them is that their shape and frequency is flexible. Furthermore, there seems to be a direct connection between changes in spines and experiences in rodents,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and dendritic spine remodelling has been established as a significant component in learning and memory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Because memory consolidation requires the stabilization of experience-dependent synaptic changes, long-term alterations in spine number, size, or morphology are thought to provide a structural substrate for the maintenance of consolidated memories.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is important as it indicates a direct relationship between experiences and neurological changes. Additionally, spine concentration on a neuron is subject to fluctuation, with that fluctuation relating to regularity of activation. Studies suggest that increased activation of neurons can lead to increases in formation of spines, while over activation can cause loss of them.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This fluidity in spine density provides a further mechanism for neurons to adapt in sensitivity. In summary, due to the combined characteristics of high flexibility of structure and function, as well as the ability to fluctuate in concentration, dendritic spines, via dendritic spine remodelling provides an immense amount of flexibility in the brain. These spine-level changes are believed to play a key role in converting transient synaptic modifications into long-lasting memory traces.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to the aforementioned processes are <a href="/wiki/Synaptogenesis" title="Synaptogenesis">synaptogenesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Synaptic_pruning" title="Synaptic pruning">synaptic pruning</a>. Synaptogenesis is the process by which a two neurons form a new synapse,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and synaptic pruning is the process by which a synaptic connection between two neurons is eliminated.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In short, these two mechanisms allow for changes in neural connections, facilitating processes of connection formation and connection degradation. This allows for a high level of variability in neural pathways as they can be subject to change based on different conditions. By acting as a mechanism that mediates overall synaptic changes, synaptogenesis and pruning can modify how information is routed and stored over time, thereby influencing the long-term consolidation of memories.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Myelination">Myelination</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Myelination"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Myelination" title="Myelination">Myelination</a></div>
<p>The last major mechanism of structural plasticity is <a href="/wiki/Myelination" title="Myelination">myelination</a>. In the brain, <a href="/wiki/Myelin" title="Myelin">myelin</a> operates as a sheath around neurons increasing the rate at which they transmit signals to each other. This is essential as myelin facilitates rapid processing in necessary neural systems. Myelination is the process with which neurons are wrapped by myelin. Recent research has uncovered that this process of myelination can occur throughout the lifetime of an organism.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Additionally, <a href="/wiki/Oligodendrocyte" title="Oligodendrocyte">oligodendrocytes</a>, which are the cells that comprise myelin in the brain are particularly flexible in their functions even in adults.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Current studies have established that myelin has a high rate of change in the brain, allowing the brain to optimize functioning speeds based on particular needs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Additionally, it has also been observed that <a href="/wiki/White_matter" title="White matter">white matter</a> increases can occur in tandem with skill acquisition<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or cognitive processing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This remains consistent with the previous points as white matter is primarily composed of myelin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Thus, the rapid changes in white matter associated with learning or cognition are a result of rapid changes in myelination. Systems-level memory consolidation depends on coordinated communication across multiple brain regions, thus, activity-dependent changes in myelination are thought to improve the timing and efficiency of these networks, contributing to some of the long-term stabilization of memories.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Spacing_effect">Spacing effect</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Spacing effect"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spacing_effect" title="Spacing effect">Spacing effect</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Distributed_learning" title="Distributed learning">Distributed learning</a> has been found to enhance memory consolidation, specifically for relational memory. Experimental results suggest that distributing learning over the course of 24 hours decreases the rate of forgetting compared to <a href="/wiki/Spacing_effect" title="Spacing effect">massed learning</a>, and enhances relational memory consolidation. When interpreted in the context of synaptic consolidation, mechanisms of synaptic strengthening may depend on the spacing of memory reactivation to allow sufficient time for <a href="/wiki/Protein_synthesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Protein synthesis">protein synthesis</a> to occur, and thereby strengthen long-term memory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Litman&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Litman-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>One study that demonstrates this effect was conducted in 1984 by Smith and Rothkopf.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Smith&#95;Rothkopf&#95;1984&#95;pp.&#95;341–358&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Rothkopf_1984_pp._341–358-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In this experiment, subjects were sorted into three groups to test retention and learning. "Each group was taught the same 8 hour statistics class, but one group was taught the class in one day, the next over the course of four days in one room, and the last was taught the class over the course of four days in different rooms. The subjects were tested five days later in a completely new setting. The results of the experiment were that taking the class over a period of four days was much more effective than taking it in one single mass. Interestingly, the group that took the course over four days and in different rooms performed the best in the final retention test out of all the groups."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Smith&#95;Rothkopf&#95;1984&#95;pp.&#95;341–358&#95;40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Rothkopf_1984_pp._341–358-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  This shows that spacing out study sessions and studying in different environments helps with retention as it provides time for the brain to consolidate the information without being interrupted by new information. The benefits of spacing were also demonstrated in an earlier study by Reder and Anderson (1982) which yielded similar results, confirming the spacing effect's relevance and effects on learning.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Goldstein&#95;2014&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldstein_2014-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Protein_synthesis">Protein synthesis</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Protein synthesis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Protein_synthesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Protein synthesis">Protein synthesis</a> plays an important role in the formation of new <a href="/wiki/Memories" class="mw-redirect" title="Memories">memories</a>. Studies have shown that <a href="/wiki/Protein_synthesis_inhibitor" title="Protein synthesis inhibitor">protein synthesis inhibitors</a> administered after <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a>, weaken memory, suggesting that protein synthesis is required for memory consolidation. Additionally, reports have suggested that the effects of protein synthesis inhibitors also inhibit <a href="/wiki/Long-term_potentiation" title="Long-term potentiation">LTP</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gold&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gold-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, other results have shown that protein synthesis may not in fact be necessary for memory consolidation, as it has been found that the formation of memories can withstand vast amounts of <a href="/wiki/Protein_synthesis_inhibitor" title="Protein synthesis inhibitor">protein synthesis inhibition</a>, suggesting that this criterion of protein synthesis as necessary for memory consolidation is not unconditional.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gold&#95;43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gold-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Systems_consolidation">Systems consolidation</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Systems consolidation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Systems consolidation is the second form of <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a> consolidation. It is a reorganization process in which memories from the <a href="/wiki/Hippocampal" class="mw-redirect" title="Hippocampal">hippocampal</a> region, where memories are first <a href="/wiki/Encoding_(memory)" title="Encoding (memory)">encoded</a>, are moved to the <a href="/wiki/Neo-cortex" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-cortex">neo-cortex</a> in a more permanent form of storage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dudai&#95;et&#95;al.&#95;2015&#95;9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dudai_et_al._2015-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Roediger&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roediger-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Systems consolidation is a slow dynamic process that can take anywhere from one to two decades to be fully formed in humans, unlike synaptic consolidation that only takes minutes to hours for new information to stabilize into memories.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Roediger&#95;44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roediger-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Standard_model_2">Standard model</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Standard model"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The standard model of systems consolidation has been summarized by Squire and Alvarez (1995);<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Squire&#95;1995&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squire_1995-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> it states that when novel information is originally <a href="/wiki/Encoding_(memory)" title="Encoding (memory)">encoded</a> and registered, memory of these new <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_(physiology)" title="Stimulus (physiology)">stimuli</a> becomes retained in both the <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">hippocampus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">cortical</a> regions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Frankland&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frankland-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Later the hippocampus' representations of this information become active in <a href="/wiki/Explicit_memory" title="Explicit memory">explicit</a> (conscious) recall or <a href="/wiki/Implicit_memory" title="Implicit memory">implicit</a> (unconscious) recall like in <a href="/wiki/Sleep" title="Sleep">sleep</a> and 'offline' processes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dudai&#95;1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dudai-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Memory is retained in the <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">hippocampus</a> for up to one week after initial <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a>, representing the hippocampus-dependent stage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Frankland&#95;46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frankland-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During this stage the hippocampus is 'teaching' the cortex more and more about the information and when the information is recalled it strengthens the cortico-cortical connection thus making the memory hippocampus-independent.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dudai&#95;1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dudai-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Therefore, from one week and beyond the initial <a href="/wiki/Training" title="Training">training</a> experience, the memory is slowly transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Neo-cortex" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-cortex">neo-cortex</a> where it becomes permanently stored.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dudai&#95;1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dudai-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In this view the hippocampus can perform the task of storing memories temporarily because the synapses are able to change quickly whereas the neocortical synapses change over time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Squire&#95;1995&#95;45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squire_1995-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Consolidation is thus the process whereby the hippocampus activates the neocortex continually leading to strong connections between the two. Since the hippocampus can only support memories temporarily the remaining activation will be seen only in the neocortex which is able to support memory indefinitely. Squire and Alvarez took the temporally graded nature of patients with retrograde amnesia as support for the notion that once a connection has been established within the neocortex the hippocampus is no longer required, but this process is dynamic and extends for several years.
</p><p>Squire and Alvarez also proposed the idea that MTL structures play a role in the consolidation of memories within the neocortex by providing a <a href="/wiki/Binding_problem" title="Binding problem">binding</a> area for multiple <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">cortical</a> <a href="/wiki/Regions" class="mw-redirect" title="Regions">regions</a> involved in the initial encoding of the memory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Squire&#95;1995&#95;45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squire_1995-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In this sense the MTL would act as a relay station for the various perceptual input that make up a memory and stores it as a whole event. After this has occurred the MTL directs information towards the neocortex to provide a permanent representation of the memory.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Multiple_trace_theory">Multiple trace theory</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Multiple trace theory"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Multiple trace theory (MTT) builds on the distinction between <a href="/wiki/Semantic_memory" title="Semantic memory">semantic memory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Episodic_memory" title="Episodic memory">episodic memory</a> and addresses perceived shortcomings of the standard model with respect to the dependency of the hippocampus. MTT argues that the hippocampus is always involved in the retrieval and storage of episodic memories.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NadelMoscovitch&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NadelMoscovitch-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is thought that semantic memories, including basic information encoded during the storage of episodic memories, can be established in structures apart from the hippocampal system such as the neo-cortex in the process of consolidation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NadelMoscovitch&#95;47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NadelMoscovitch-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Hence, while proper hippocampal functioning is necessary for the retention and retrieval of episodic memories, it is less necessary during the encoding and use of semantic memories. As memories age there are long-term interactions between the hippocampus and neo-cortex and this leads to the establishment of aspects of memory within structures aside from the hippocampus.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NadelMoscovitch&#95;47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NadelMoscovitch-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> MTT thus states that both episodic and semantic memories rely on the hippocampus and the latter becomes somewhat independent of the hippocampus during consolidation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NadelMoscovitch&#95;47-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NadelMoscovitch-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
An important distinction between MTT and the standard model is that the standard model proposes that all memories become independent of the hippocampus after several years. However, Nadel and Moscovitch have shown that the hippocampus was involved in memory recall for all remote <a href="/wiki/Autobiographical" class="mw-redirect" title="Autobiographical">autobiographical</a> memories no matter of their age.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NadelMoscovitch&#95;47-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NadelMoscovitch-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An important point they make while interpreting the results is that activation in the hippocampus was equally as strong regardless of the fact that the memories recalled were as old as 45 years prior to the date of the experiment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NadelMoscovitch&#95;47-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NadelMoscovitch-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is complicated by the fact that the hippocampus is constantly involved in the encoding of new events and activation due to this fact is hard to separate using baseline measures.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NadelMoscovitch&#95;47-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NadelMoscovitch-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Because of this, activation of the hippocampus during retrieval of distant memories may simply be a <a href="/wiki/By-product" title="By-product">by-product</a> of the subject encoding the study as an event.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NadelMoscovitch&#95;47-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NadelMoscovitch-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Criticisms">Criticisms</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Criticisms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Haist, Gore, and Mao, sought to examine the temporal nature of consolidation within the hippocampus to test MTT against the standard view.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Haist&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haist-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They found that the hippocampus does not substantially contribute to the recollection of remote memories after a period of a few years. They claim that advances in the functional magnetic resonance imaging have allowed them to improve their distinction between the hippocampus and the entorhinal cortex which they claim is more enduring in its activation from remote memory retrieval.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Haist&#95;48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haist-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They also criticize the use of memories during testing which cannot be confirmed as accurate.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Haist&#95;48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haist-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Finally, they state that the initial interview in the scanner acted as an encoding event as such differences between recent and remote memories would be obscured.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Haist&#95;48-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haist-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Semantic_vs._episodic_memory">Semantic vs. episodic memory</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Semantic vs. episodic memory"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Nadel and Moscovitch argued that when studying the structures and systems involved in <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a> consolidation, <a href="/wiki/Semantic_memory" title="Semantic memory">semantic memory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Episodic_memory" title="Episodic memory">episodic memory</a> need to be distinguished as relying on two different memory systems. When episodic information is encoded there are semantic aspects of the memory that are encoded as well and this is proposed as an explanation of the varying gradients of memory loss seen in amnesic patients.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NadelMoscovitch&#95;47-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NadelMoscovitch-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Amnesic patients with hippocampal damage show traces of memories and this has been used as support for the standard model because it suggests that memories are retained apart from the hippocampal system.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NadelMoscovitch&#95;47-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NadelMoscovitch-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Nadel and Moscovitch argue that these retained memories have lost the richness of experience and exist as depersonalized events that have been semanticized over time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NadelMoscovitch&#95;47-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NadelMoscovitch-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They suggest that this instead provides support for their notion that episodic memories rely significantly on the hippocampal system but semantic memories can be established elsewhere in the brain and survive hippocampal damage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NadelMoscovitch&#95;47-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NadelMoscovitch-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Declarative_vs._procedural_knowledge_consolidation">Declarative vs. procedural knowledge consolidation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Declarative vs. procedural knowledge consolidation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Learning can be distinguished by two forms of knowledge: <a href="/wiki/Declarative_memory" class="mw-redirect" title="Declarative memory">declarative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Procedural_memory" title="Procedural memory">procedural</a>. Declarative information includes the <a href="/wiki/Conscious" class="mw-redirect" title="Conscious">conscious</a> recall of facts, episodes, and lists, and its storage typically connected with the mediotemporal lobe and the hippocampal systems as it includes the encoding of both semantic and episodic information of events. <a href="/wiki/Procedural_knowledge" title="Procedural knowledge">Procedural knowledge</a> however has been said to function separate from this system as it relies primarily on motor areas of the brain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Squire&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squire-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The implicit nature of procedural knowledge allows it to exist absent from the conscious awareness that the information is there. Amnesic patients have shown retained ability to be trained on tasks and exhibit learning without the subject being aware that the training had ever taken place.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Squire&#95;49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squire-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This introduces a dissociation between the two forms of memory and the fact that one form can exist absent the other suggests separate mechanisms are involved in consolidation. Squire has proposed the procedural knowledge is consolidated in some cases by the extrapyramidal motor system.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Squire&#95;49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squire-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Squire demonstrated that intact learning of certain motor, perceptual, and cognitive skills can be retained in patients with amnesia.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Squire&#95;49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squire-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They also retain the ability to be influenced by priming effects without the patients being able to consciously recall any training session occurring.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Squire&#95;49-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squire-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Emotional_and_stressful_memory_consolidation">Emotional and stressful memory consolidation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Emotional and stressful memory consolidation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">amygdala</a>, specifically the <a href="/wiki/Basolateral_amygdala" title="Basolateral amygdala">basolateral region</a> (BLA) is involved in the encoding of significant experiences and has been directly linked to memorable events.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-JamesLMcGaugh&#95;6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JamesLMcGaugh-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Extensive evidence suggests that stress hormones such as epinephrine play a critical role in consolidating new memories and this is why stressful memories are recalled vividly.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-JamesLMcGaugh2&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JamesLMcGaugh2-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Studies by Gold and van Buskirk provided initial evidence for this relationship when they showed that injections of epinephrine into subjects following a training period resulted in greater long-term retention of task related memories.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-GoldVanBuskirk&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldVanBuskirk-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gold2&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gold2-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This study also provided evidence that the level of epinephrine injected was related to the level of retention suggesting that the level of stress or emotionality of the memory plays a role on the level of retention. It is suggested that epinephrine affects memory consolidation by activating the amygdala and studies have shown that <a href="/wiki/Antagonist" title="Antagonist">antagonism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Adrenergic_receptor" title="Adrenergic receptor">beta-andrenoreceptors</a> prior to <a href="/wiki/Injection_(medicine)" title="Injection (medicine)">injection</a> of <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine" class="mw-redirect" title="Epinephrine">epinephrine</a> will block the retention of memory effects seen previously.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Liang&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liang-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Liang2&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liang2-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is supported by the fact that beta-adrenoreceptor <a href="/wiki/Agonists" class="mw-redirect" title="Agonists">agonists</a> have the opposite effect on the enhancement of memory consolidation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Liang&#95;53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liang-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Liang2&#95;54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liang2-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The BLA is thought to be actively involved in memory consolidation and is influenced strongly by stress hormones resulting in increased activation and as such increased memory retention.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-JamesLMcGaugh2&#95;50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JamesLMcGaugh2-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The BLA then projects to the hippocampus resulting in a strengthened memory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-JamesLMcGaugh&#95;6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JamesLMcGaugh-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This relationship was studied by Packard and Chen who found that when <a href="/wiki/Glutamate" class="mw-redirect" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a> was administered to the hippocampus, enhanced consolidation was seen during food-rewarded maze tasks.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Packard&#95;55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Packard-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The opposite effect was also seen when the amygdala was inactivated using <a href="/wiki/Lidocaine" title="Lidocaine">lidocaine</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Packard&#95;55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Packard-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Studies appear to suggest that the amygdala effects the consolidation of memories through its influence with stress hormones and the projections to other brain areas implicated in memory consolidation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-JamesLMcGaugh&#95;6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JamesLMcGaugh-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sleep_consolidation">Sleep consolidation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Sleep consolidation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sleep_and_memory" title="Sleep and memory">Sleep and memory</a></div>
<p>During sleep, newly encoded memories are stabilized and reorganized with the help of the interactions between the neocortex and the hippocampus. <sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Early research focused on <a href="/wiki/Rem_sleep" class="mw-redirect" title="Rem sleep">Rapid eye movement</a> (REM) <a href="/wiki/Sleep" title="Sleep">sleep</a> as a key important stage for memory consolidation by establishing information in the <a href="/wiki/Hippocampal" class="mw-redirect" title="Hippocampal">hippocampal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">cortical</a> regions of the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">brain</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Walker&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> REM sleep elicits an increase in <a href="/wiki/Neuronal" class="mw-redirect" title="Neuronal">neuronal</a> activity following an enriched or novel waking experience, thus increasing neuronal plasticity and therefore playing an essential role in the consolidation of memories.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Riberio&#95;58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riberio-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This has come into question in recent years however and studies on <a href="/wiki/Sleep_deprivation" title="Sleep deprivation">sleep deprivation</a> have shown that animals and humans who are denied REM sleep do not show deficits in task learning. It has been proposed that since the brain is in a non-memory encoding state during sleep, consolidation would be unlikely to occur.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Vertes&#95;59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vertes-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>More recent studies, however, have suggested slow-wave sleep plays a central role on memory consolidation rather than REM sleep. A review on sleep related memory consolidation methods looked into a combination of the coordination between thalamocortical sleep spindles, hippocampal sharp-wave ripples and cortical slow oscillations to explain the transfer of new memories from the Hippocampus to neocortical networks supporting long-term memory stabilization.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One study found that low levels of acetylcholine found in the central nervous system, which are present during slow-wave sleep, aid in the consolidation of memories and therefore assist in the learning process.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gais&#95;Hüllemann&#95;Hallschmid&#95;Born&#95;2006&#95;pp.&#95;786–791&#95;61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gais_Hüllemann_Hallschmid_Born_2006_pp._786–791-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Recent studies have examined the relationship between REM sleep and procedural learning consolidation.  
In particular studies have been done on <a href="/wiki/Sensory_perception" class="mw-redirect" title="Sensory perception">sensory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Motor_skill" title="Motor skill">motor</a> related tasks. In one study testing finger-tapping, people were split into two groups and tested post-training with or without intervening sleep; results concluded that sleep post-training increases both speed and accuracy in this particular task, while increasing the activation of both cortical and <a href="/wiki/Hippocampal" class="mw-redirect" title="Hippocampal">hippocampal</a> regions; whereas the post-training awake group had no such improvements.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Walker&#95;57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has been theorized that this may be related more-so to a process of synaptic consolidation rather than systems consolidation because of the short-term nature of the process involved.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Vertes&#95;59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vertes-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Researchers examining the effect of sleep on motor learning have noted that while consolidation occurs over a period of 4–6 hours during sleep, this is also true during waking hours, which may negate any role of sleep in learning.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Vertes&#95;59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vertes-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In this sense sleep would serve no special purpose to enhance consolidation of memories because it occurs independently of sleep.
Other studies have examined the process of replay which has been described as a reactivation of patterns that were stimulated during a learning phase. Replay has been demonstrated in the hippocampus and this has lent support to the notion that it serves a consolidation purpose.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Vertes&#95;59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vertes-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, replay is not specific to sleep and both rats and primates show signs during restful-awake periods.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Vertes&#95;59-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vertes-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Also, replay may simply be residual activation in areas that were involved previously in the learning phase and may have no actual effect on consolidation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Vertes&#95;59-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vertes-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This reactivation of the memory traces has also been seen in non-REM sleep specifically for hippocampus-dependent memories.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wamsley&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wamsley-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  New evidence further suggests that REM sleep contributes to memory consolidation by facilitating selective reactivation of memory traces, which can either strengthen or weaken them.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Researchers have also noted strong reactivation of the hippocampus during sleep immediately after a learning task. This reactivation led to enhanced performance on the learned task.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wamsley&#95;63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wamsley-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One such experiment had participants learn word pair associations (declarative memories) before either retention periods of sleep or periods of wakefulness. Researchers found that retrieval expectancy played a role as to whether participants were able to retain the information, as the participants that had been told about the delayed retrieval test performed better. However, their research showed that sleep was more likely to benefit the consolidation of memories if the information was relevant to future events or behaviors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Researchers following this line of work have come to assume that <a href="/wiki/Dream" title="Dream">dreams</a> are a by-product of the reactivation of the brain areas and this can explain why dreams may be unrelated to the information being consolidated.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wamsley&#95;63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wamsley-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The dream experience itself is not what enhances memory performance but rather it is the reactivation of the neural circuits that causes this. Other researchers have looked at the role growth hormones play in the consolidation of memories, particularly those of procedural and declarative memories. They found that although growth hormones support general brain systems and memory functioning, it is still unclear if growth hormones play a role in the formation and processing of particular memories during sleep periods.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gais&#95;Hüllemann&#95;Hallschmid&#95;Born&#95;2006&#95;pp.&#95;786–791&#95;61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gais_Hüllemann_Hallschmid_Born_2006_pp._786–791-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Cortical_slow_oscillation-_and_spindle-complexes">Cortical slow oscillation- and spindle-complexes</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Cortical slow oscillation- and spindle-complexes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Memory consolidation during sleep via reactivation of prior experiences and information is associated with sleep signatures of cortical "slow <a href="/wiki/Neural_oscillation" title="Neural oscillation">oscillations</a>" and <a href="/wiki/Sleep_spindle" title="Sleep spindle">sleep spindles</a> that are involved in the information flow between relevant brain areas. These oscillatory events coordinate the timing of these interactions in order to facilitate the transfer of information from the hippocampus to the neocortex.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A more complete understanding of these mechanics may possibly allow purposely enabling or strengthening this reactivation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Zif268_and_REM_sleep">Zif268 and REM sleep</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Zif268 and REM sleep"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Zif268" class="mw-redirect" title="Zif268">Zif268</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Immediate_early_gene" title="Immediate early gene">immediate early gene</a> (IEG) thought to be involved in neuroplasticity by an <a href="/wiki/Up-regulation" class="mw-redirect" title="Up-regulation">up-regulation</a> of the transcription factor during <a href="/wiki/REM_sleep" class="mw-redirect" title="REM sleep">REM sleep</a> after pre-exposure to an enriched environment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Riberio&#95;58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riberio-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Results from studies testing the effects of zif268 on mice brains <a href="/wiki/Postmortem" class="mw-redirect" title="Postmortem">postmortem</a>, suggest that a waking experience prior to sleep can have an enduring effect in the brain, due to an increase of neuroplasticity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Riberio&#95;58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riberio-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Reconsolidation">Reconsolidation</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Reconsolidation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Memory reconsolidation is the process of previously consolidated memories being recalled and actively consolidated.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tronson&#95;15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tronson-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is a distinct process that serves to maintain, strengthen and modify <a href="/wiki/Memories" class="mw-redirect" title="Memories">memories</a> that are already stored in the <a href="/wiki/Long-term_memory" title="Long-term memory">long-term memory</a>. Once memories undergo the process of consolidation and become part of long-term memory, they are thought of as stable. However, the retrieval of a <a href="/wiki/Memory_trace" class="mw-redirect" title="Memory trace">memory trace</a> can cause another <a href="/wiki/Labile" class="mw-redirect" title="Labile">labile</a> phase that then requires an active process to make the <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a> stable after retrieval is complete.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tronson&#95;15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tronson-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  It is believed that post-retrieval stabilization is different and distinct from consolidation, despite its overlap in function (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Storage_(memory)" title="Storage (memory)">storage</a>) and its mechanisms (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Protein_synthesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Protein synthesis">protein synthesis</a>). Memory modification needs to be demonstrated in the retrieval in order for this independent process to be valid.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tronson&#95;15-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tronson-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="History_2">History</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The theory of reconsolidation has been debated for many years and is still controversial. Reconsolidation was first conceptualized in light of the discovery that <a href="/wiki/Phobias" class="mw-redirect" title="Phobias">phobias</a> could often be eliminated by means of <a href="/wiki/Electroconvulsive_shock_therapy" class="mw-redirect" title="Electroconvulsive shock therapy">electroconvulsive shock therapy</a> (ECT).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This seemed to indicate the involvement of a re-consolidation process for excited memories, and that the operation active in ECT was the disruption of that process; here, of the reconsolidation of retrieved fear memories by shock administration.
</p><p>Further studies investigated the concept,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tronson&#95;15-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tronson-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> using ECT to test for reconsolidation; ECT was already known as an <a href="/wiki/Amnesic" class="mw-redirect" title="Amnesic">amnesic</a> agent (leads to memory loss). These studies found it to be effective on retrieved memories when administered directly after the retrieval of a memory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dudai&#95;1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dudai-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
