</p><p>Later research, wherein fear memories had been established in rats through <a href="/wiki/Pavlovian" class="mw-redirect" title="Pavlovian">Pavlovian</a> <a href="/wiki/Fear_conditioning" title="Fear conditioning">fear conditioning</a>, found that a consolidated fear memory can be brought to a <a href="/wiki/Labile" class="mw-redirect" title="Labile">labile</a> state, by means of immediate <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">amygdala</a> infusions of the <a href="/wiki/Protein_synthesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Protein synthesis">protein synthesis</a> inhibitor <a href="/wiki/Anisomycin" title="Anisomycin">anisomycin</a>, but not by infusions made six hours afterwards.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nader&#95;4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nader-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It was concluded that consolidated fear memory, when reactivated, enters a changeable state that requires <i>de novo</i> <a href="/wiki/Protein_synthesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Protein synthesis">protein synthesis</a> for new consolidation, i.e., re-consolidation of the old memory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nader&#95;4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nader-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Nader, Schafe, and Le Doux (2000) demonstrated that the reconsolidation  process may make memories more malleable than previously believed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Goldstein&#95;2014&#95;42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldstein_2014-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Nader and his colleagues trained rats to be afraid of a tone by pairing the tone with a small shock. Groups of rats were then injected with anisomycin, an antibiotic that restricts protein synthesis, at different points in time. The rats that were injected with anisomycin after consolidation had taken place, retained the fear reaction to the tone. However, the rats that were injected before consolidation and reconsolidation could take place, did not retain the fear response when they heard the tone again later. It seems that interference that is made before memories are consolidated affect the way they are remembered later. 
</p><p>Brunet and colleagues (2008) studied patients that had been diagnosed with Post Traumatic Stress Disorder (<a href="/wiki/Posttraumatic_stress_disorder" class="mw-redirect" title="Posttraumatic stress disorder">PTSD</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Following the same method that Nader and his associates used, Brunet induced anxiety responses in the patients by having them listen to a 30 second recording describing the circumstances of their traumatic experiences. The patients were shortly thereafter injected with propranolol, a drug that blocks stress hormone receptors in the amygdala which is implicated in neurologically representing the emotional content of memories. These patients experienced a significant reduction in PTSD symptoms months after treatment. These findings were confirmed in later studies done in 2009 by Kindt and colleagues and in 2010 by Schiller and colleagues.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>These studies done by Nader and others seem to suggest that as memories are being remembered, they are fragile, as if experiencing them for the first time.  
</p><p>In addition to fear memories, appetitive memories are also prone to reconsolidation episodes, which can likewise be disrupted; namely, after local administration of a protein activity inhibitor.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-drug&#95;memory&#95;74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-drug_memory-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Since those breakthrough studies were done, there have been several others to probe the theory of memory reconsolidation. Subjects in these studies, along with <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>, have included <a href="/wiki/Crabs" class="mw-redirect" title="Crabs">crabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chicks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honeybees" class="mw-redirect" title="Honeybees">honeybees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oryzias_latipes" class="mw-redirect" title="Oryzias latipes">medaka fish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lymnaea" title="Lymnaea">lymnaea</a>, and various <a href="/wiki/Rodents" class="mw-redirect" title="Rodents">rodents</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tronson&#95;15-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tronson-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Further studies have demonstrated an analogue of memory reconsolidation in spinal cord pain processing pathways, suggesting a general role for reconsolidation in the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pain&#95;reconsolidation&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pain_reconsolidation-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Criticisms_2">Criticisms</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Criticisms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Some studies have supported this theory, while others have failed to demonstrate disruption of consolidated memory after retrieval. Negative results may be examples of conditions where memories are not susceptible to a permanent disruption, thus a determining factor of reconsolidation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tronson&#95;15-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tronson-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After much debate and a detailed review of this field it had been concluded that reconsolidation was a real phenomenon.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sara&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sara-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Tronson and Taylor compiled a lengthy summary of multiple reconsolidation studies, noting a number of studies were unable to show memory impairments due to blocked reconsolidation.  However the need for standardized methods was underscored as in some learning tasks such as <a href="/wiki/Fear_conditioning" title="Fear conditioning">fear conditioning</a>, certain forms of memory reactivation could actually represent new <a href="/wiki/Extinction_(psychology)" title="Extinction (psychology)">extinction</a> <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a> rather than activation of an old <a href="/wiki/Memory_trace" class="mw-redirect" title="Memory trace">memory trace</a>.  Under this possibility, traditional disruptions of reconsolidation might actually maintain the original memory trace but prevent the consolidation of <a href="/wiki/Extinction_(psychology)" title="Extinction (psychology)">extinction</a> <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tronson&#95;15-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tronson-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Recent work has suggested that <a href="/wiki/Epigenetics" title="Epigenetics">epigenetic</a> modifications may also prevent reconsolidation in some cases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-HDAC&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDAC-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The removal of these epigenetic modifications with inhibitors of <a href="/wiki/Histone_deacetylase" title="Histone deacetylase">histone deacetylase</a> enabled the erasure of remote memories after recall.
</p><p>Reconsolidation <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a> are more difficult to run than typical consolidation experiments as disruption of a previously consolidated memory must be shown to be specific to the reactivation of the original <a href="/wiki/Memory_trace" class="mw-redirect" title="Memory trace">memory trace</a>.  Furthermore, it is important to demonstrate that the <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability" title="Vulnerability">vulnerability</a> of reactivation occurs in a limited time frame, which can be assessed by delaying <a href="/wiki/Infusion_pump" title="Infusion pump">infusion</a> till six hours after reactivation.  It is also useful to show that the <a href="/wiki/Behavioral" class="mw-redirect" title="Behavioral">behavioral</a> measure used to assess disruption of <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a> is not just due to task impairment caused by the procedure, which can be demonstrated by testing control groups in absence of the original <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a>.  Finally, it is important to rule out alternative explanations, such as <a href="/wiki/Extinction_(psychology)" title="Extinction (psychology)">extinction</a> <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a> by lengthening the reactivation phase.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tronson&#95;15-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tronson-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There have also been concerns about the use of reconsolidation research to justify psychotherapy treatments, and the generalizability of basic reconsolidation research into the therapy room<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Distinctions_from_consolidation">Distinctions from consolidation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Distinctions from consolidation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Questions arose if reconsolidation was a unique process or merely another phase of consolidation.  Both consolidation and reconsolidation can be disrupted by pharmacological agents (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Translation_(genetics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Translation (genetics)">protein synthesis</a> inhibitor <a href="/wiki/Anisomycin" title="Anisomycin">anisomycin</a>) and both require the <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factor</a> <a href="/wiki/CREB" title="CREB">CREB</a>. However, recent amygdala research suggests that <a href="/wiki/BDNF" class="mw-redirect" title="BDNF">BDNF</a> is required for consolidation (but not reconsolidation) whereas the <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Immediate_early_genes" class="mw-redirect" title="Immediate early genes">immediate early gene</a> <a href="/wiki/Zif268" class="mw-redirect" title="Zif268">Zif268</a> is required for reconsolidation but not consolidation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  A similar <a href="/wiki/Double_dissociation" class="mw-redirect" title="Double dissociation">double dissociation</a> between <a href="/wiki/Zif268" class="mw-redirect" title="Zif268">Zif268</a> for reconsolidation and <a href="/wiki/BDNF" class="mw-redirect" title="BDNF">BDNF</a> for consolidation was found in the <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">hippocampus</a> for <a href="/wiki/Fear_conditioning" title="Fear conditioning">fear conditioning</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, not all <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a> tasks show this <a href="/wiki/Double_dissociation" class="mw-redirect" title="Double dissociation">double dissociation</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Object_recognition" class="mw-redirect" title="Object recognition">object recognition</a> memory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Reconsolidation_in_psychotherapy">Reconsolidation in psychotherapy</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory_consolidation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Reconsolidation in psychotherapy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In the decade between 2005 and 2015, at least five groups argued the notion that memory reconsolidation can be used to treat psychological problems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Welling2012&#95;83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welling2012-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lane2015&#95;84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lane2015-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Three of these groups have proposed that the wide variety of different psychotherapies produce permanent change in clients to the extent that they manage to activate this same neurobiological mechanism of reconsolidation in a way that leads to deconsolidation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Welling2012&#95;83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welling2012-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lane2015&#95;84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lane2015-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ecker2012&#95;86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ecker2012-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One example of this is the Lefkoe Method, created in 1985 by Morty Lefkoe, president and founder of the Lefkoe Institute.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Memory reconsolidation may be a <a href="/wiki/Common_factors_theory" title="Common factors theory">common factor</a> in <a href="/wiki/Integrative_psychotherapy" title="Integrative psychotherapy">many forms of psychotherapy</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ecker2012&#95;86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ecker2012-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Another approach, known as Memrec, is a treatment protocol developed by researchers at the University of Amsterdam. It combines a brief reactivation of fear memory with administration of the beta-blocker propranolol, aiming to disrupt the reconsolidation process. Clinical studies have shown reduction in fear responses after a single session in patients with specific phobias.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Soeter2015&#95;90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soeter2015-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite&#95;note-Ecker2012-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Ecker2012_86-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Ecker2012_86-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFEckerTicicHulley2012" class="citation book cs1">Ecker, Bruce; Ticic, Robin; Hulley, Laurel (2012). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=fQpd13K0gHUC"><i>Unlocking the Emotional Brain: Eliminating Symptoms at Their Roots Using Memory Reconsolidation</i></a>.
